The ninth study: Biographies of prominent figures in the commentary. 171. H. Also: Their statements have unanimously agreed that Abu Hanifa was among the jurists, to the extent that Muhammad ibn Idris al-Imam al-Shafi'i said about him: 'People are dependent on Abu Hanifa in fiqh.' And no historian or hadith scholar mentioned him except that they described him as the jurist of the people of Iraq. And it is known that this attribute cannot exist without the strength of ijtihad, for it is a condition for obtaining fiqh to have the faculty of deduction and ijtihad, as is explicitly stated in the books of usul al-fiqh. Therefore, they explicitly stated that a muqallid who does not have the faculty of deduction is not a faqih, but rather a narrator and transmitter. So if he only had seventeen hadiths, how could their judgment be correct, and how could al-Shafi'i's judgment about him be correct in that matter? 6. Also: The subsidiary issues in worship and transactions that are transmitted from the Imam in the books of his students, such as the six books of Imam Muhammad: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Mabsut, and al-Ziyadat, and his Kitab al-Athar, and his Kitab al-Hujaj, and Kitab al-Kharaj of Abu Yusuf, and al-Amali of his, and al-Mujarrad of Ibn Ziyad, and the like, are more than can be counted. And all of them are not explicitly stated in the Qur'an, nor established by consensus, and most of them cannot be grasped by mere qiyas and opinion. If he did not have hadiths reaching him, how could he give fatwa about them, and from where did he extract them, and judge by them? And one who only has seventeen hadiths reaching him, how could he give fatwa about these numerous rulings? If you say: It is possible that his auditions were only seventeen, and he came across many hadiths without narration, and extracted rulings from them. I say: The hadith books were not compiled in his time, and there was no way to access hadiths except by hearing from the mouths of the bearers of the Sharia. Also: His teachers in knowledge, as mentioned by Ibn Hajar and others, are four thousand, and among them counted in Tahdhib al-Kamal (1) and other books of critical scholars of men are about seventy teachers. If he heard only one hadith from each of his teachers, his narrations would reach seventy or four thousand. So what is the meaning of them being seventeen? Also: One who only has seventeen hadiths reaching him is not counted among the muhaddithun, let alone being included in the ranks of the precise memorizers (huffaz), while they counted him among the huffaz, as is not hidden from one who reads Tadhkirat al-Huffaz. If you say: His inclusion among the huffaz does not prove that he was actually a hafiz in reality. I say: Then the trustworthiness of the sayings of the critical scholars of men, such as al-Dhahabi, Ibn Hajar, al-Mizzi, and others among the masters of perfection, would be nullified, because such a possibility exists for everyone they have counted among the huffaz of hadith, and they have investigated their conditions with thorough investigation. 9. Also: The speech of Ibn Khaldun after mentioning a phrase in which this word occurred, which we previously quoted in the discussion of the paucity of narration, is evidence that it is not from him or that it was a slip from him, for he testified therein that Abu Hanifa is among the great mujtahids in hadith. If he thought that only seventeen hadiths reached him, this testimony would not be valid from him. In summary: That word, meaning 'his narrations reached seventeen,' has been belied by Ibn Khaldun's own phrase, and belied by the phrases of others, and the indication of the consensus of hadith scholars and historians has witnessed its falsehood, and the perusal of the books of Abu Hanifa and his precise students proclaimed it to be a mistake, and the perusal of the words of other mujtahids judged its non-acceptance. Despite all this, only a despicable aggressor believes it, not an intelligent person. Its example is like one saying about al-Bukhari, the chief of the muhaddithun, that only three or twenty hadiths reached him, and that he was not among the jurists nor among the mujtahids at all. There is no doubt that such words, whose falsehood is witnessed by the testimony of existence and the evidence of consensus, and which reason and transmission judge to be a mistake without defense, are not accepted by anyone without dispute. So preserve all this, for it will benefit you in your worldly and afterlife affairs. As for the praise of people upon him, and their testimony to his ijtihad in worship, his piety, his scrupulousness, his degree in obedience, and other merits and qualities of distinction, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi has mentioned them in his History (1), and al-Nawawi (2), and Ibn H... (Y)~ (1) Tarikh Baghdad (10:152-165). (2) In Tahdhib al-Asma' wa al-Lughat (2:216-223).
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a biographical defense of Imam Abu Hanifa by the author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya.' It addresses and refutes a false claim that Abu Hanifa only knew seventeen hadiths. The author presents multiple arguments (adillah) to demonstrate the absurdity of this claim.

The first argument (point هـ) is the unanimous consensus (ijma) of historians and hadith scholars that Abu Hanifa was a faqih (jurist) of the highest caliber, even praised by Imam al-Shafi'i as the one upon whom people depend in fiqh. The author asserts that being a faqih requires the faculty of ijtihad (independent legal reasoning) and istinbat (deduction). A mere muqallid (follower) who lacks this faculty is not truly a faqih. Therefore, if Abu Hanifa had only seventeen hadiths, he could not have been a faqih, which contradicts the consensus.

The second argument (point 6) points to the vast number of legal rulings (masa'il) attributed to Abu Hanifa in the books of his students, such as the six books of Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah) and others. These rulings cover worship and transactions, and many are not explicitly found in the Qur'an or established by consensus (ijma), nor can they be derived purely by qiyas (analogy) or opinion. They must be based on hadith. If Abu Hanifa had only seventeen hadiths, he could not have derived these countless rulings.

The author then anticipates an objection (fa-in qulta) that perhaps Abu Hanifa knew many hadiths through avenues other than direct narration. He refutes this by noting that hadith books were not yet compiled in Abu Hanifa's time; the only way to access hadiths was through oral transmission from teachers. He then cites historical evidence that Abu Hanifa had approximately four thousand teachers (shuyukh), with about seventy specifically mentioned in Tahdhib al-Kamal. Even if he heard just one hadith from each teacher, his collection would be in the thousands. The number seventeen is thus impossible.

The third argument (point 7) notes that a person with only seventeen hadiths would not be considered a muhaddith (hadith scholar), let alone a hafiz (memorizer). Yet the consensus of hadith scholars, including al-Dhahabi, Ibn Hajar, and al-Mizzi, list Abu Hanifa among the huffaz. Accepting the claim would undermine the credibility of these critical scholars.

The fourth argument (point 9) cites Ibn Khaldun's testimony that Abu Hanifa was a great mujtahid in hadith, which contradicts the attribution of the seventeen-hadith claim to Ibn Khaldun, suggesting it was a slip of the pen or not his true opinion.

Finally, the author concludes that this false claim is akin to saying Imam al-Bukhari only knew a handful of hadiths—a statement clearly false by the evidence of his works and the consensus of scholars. The passage ends with a note that the praiseworthy qualities of Abu Hanifa in worship and piety are well-documented in works like Tarikh Baghdad and Tahdhib al-Asma'.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning to derive rulings from primary sources; a faculty required for being a faqih.
مقلد (muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without possessing independent deduction ability; not considered a faqih.
حافظ (hafiz): A hadith scholar who has memorized a vast number of narrations; Abu Hanifa is counted among them by consensus.
نقاد الرجال (nuqqad al-rijal): Critical scholars who evaluate the reliability of hadith transmitters; their consensus is trustworthy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT used as evidence to refute the claim that Abu Hanifa only knew 17 hadiths?
A) The consensus of historians and hadith scholars that he was a faqih.
B) The fact that his students wrote numerous books containing many legal rulings.
C) The claim that he derived all his rulings solely from Qur'an and consensus.
D) The large number of his teachers, estimated at four thousand.

Key Takeaway
The false claim that Imam Abu Hanifa knew only 17 hadiths is thoroughly refuted by consensus, his extensive rulings, his teachers, his status as a hafiz, and Ibn Khaldun's testimony.

Abu Hanifa's Refusal of Judgeship
Biographical accounts of Abu Hanifa's refusal of judgeship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to narrate the two key incidents of Abu Hanifa's refusal of the judiciary and explain his reasoning based on the accounts.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa endured lashing and imprisonment rather than accept a judgeship he deemed unfit.

This lesson explores two historical accounts from the biographical section, showing Imam Abu Hanifa's unwavering refusal to take the position of qadi (judge) under both Umayyad and Abbasid rulers. Through the narrations of 'Abdullah al-Raqqi and Mu'attib, we see his profound sense of accountability and his clever argument to avoid the office.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulers
Note the different rulers: Ibn Hubayra (Umayyad) and al-Mansur (Abbasid). Both offered the judiciary.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
In the first, he refused and endured lashes; in the second, he gave a logical excuse that trapped the caliph.
3
Extract the underlying principle
Why did he refuse? The text implies it was due to piety (wara') and fear of injustice.
4
Connect to Hanafi ethos
This refusal is emblematic of the Hanafi emphasis on avoiding positions of authority unless one is fully qualified and just.

Ninth Study: Biographies of Notables in the Commentary

And al-Suyuti, al-Dhahabi, al-Yafi'i, al-Sha'rani, al-Mizzi, and other great hadith scholars and historians have a large number of these, and if collected in one volume it would be a large book. We will suffice with some of it:
From 'Abdullah al-Raqqi (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Ibn Hubayra – who was a governor of Iraq during the Umayyad period – asked Abu Hanifa to accept the judgeship of Kufa but he refused. So he struck him a hundred lashes, ten lashes each day, while he continued to refuse. When he saw that, he let him go.'
From Mu'attib who said: Kharaja ibn Badil (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Abu Ja'far al-Mansur called Abu Hanifa to the judiciary but he refused, so he imprisoned him. Then he called him and said: 'Do you dislike what we are in?' He said: 'May Allah rectify the Commander of the Faithful, I am not fit for the judiciary.' He said: 'You have lied.' Then he offered it a second time. Abu Hanifa said: 'The Commander of the Faithful has judged that I am not fit for the judiciary because he accused me of lying. If I am a liar, then I am not fit; if I am truthful, then I have informed you that I am not fit for the judiciary.'
From al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa was a well-known jurist, famous for piety, known for his kindness to those who visited him, patient in teaching knowledge by night and day, very silent and seldom speaking until a question would be posed to him.'

From Ibn al-Mubarak (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I have not seen anyone like Abu Hanifa in jurisprudence.' And from Abu Nu'aym (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa was a deep diver into issues.' And from Ja'far ibn al-Rabi' (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I stayed with Abu Hanifa for five years, and I never saw anyone more silent. When asked about something in jurisprudence, he would open up and flow like a valley.' And from Yahya ibn Ayyub (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa did not sleep at night.'

'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah
From Asad ibn 'Amr (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa prayed the Fajr prayer with the ablution of the 'Isha' prayer for forty years. He would spend most of the night reciting the Qur'an and weeping, so that his neighbors would hear his weeping. He completed the Qur'an in the place where he died seven thousand times.' And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'While I was walking with Abu Hanifa, he heard a man saying: 'This is a man who does not sleep at night.' Abu Hanifa said: 'Do not speak about me concerning what I do not do.' So he used to enliven the night with prayer, supplication, and humility.' And from Mis'ar who said: 'One night I entered the mosque and saw a man praying. He kept reciting in prayer until he completed the entire Qur'an in one rak'ah. I looked and it was Abu Hanifa.' And from Za'idah who said: 'I prayed 'Isha' with Abu Hanifa in the mosque. The people left, and he did not know that I was in the mosque. He stood and began the prayer, until he reached this verse: 'But Allah was gracious to us and protected us' (52:27). He kept repeating it until the mu'adhdhin called the adhan for Fajr prayer.' And from Waki' who said: 'Abu Hanifa was characterized by great trustworthiness. He preferred the pleasure of Allah over everything, and if swords were to take him in the way of Allah, he would bear them.'
From Ibn al-Mubarak, it was said to Sufyan al-Thawri (may Allah have mercy on him): 'How far is Abu Hanifa from backbiting! I never heard him backbite anyone, even an enemy.' He said: 'He is too intelligent to empower something that would take away his good deeds.' And from Ibrahim ibn 'Ikrimah who said: 'I have not seen in my time anyone more ascetic, more devout, or more knowledgeable than Abu Hanifa.' And from Ibn Dawud: 'If you want the narrations (athar), then [take from] Sufyan; if you want the subtleties, then Abu Hanifa.'
From al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): 'Whoever wants to be deeply learned in jurisprudence is dependent on Abu Hanifa; whoever wants to be deeply learned in campaigns (maghazi) is dependent on Muhammad ibn Ishaq; whoever wants to be deeply learned in grammar is dependent on al-Kisa'i.'
From Ibn Ma'in: 'The recitation (qira'ah) according to me is the recitation of Hamzah, and the jurisprudence is the jurisprudence of Abu Hanifa.' And from 'Ali ibn 'Asim: 'If the intellect of Abu Hanifa were weighed against the intellect of all the people of the earth, it would outweigh them.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is the ninth study on biographies of notable figures, presenting a series of narrations (riwayat) about the character and virtues of Imam Abu Hanifa. The author cites multiple sources to establish Abu Hanifa's piety, knowledge, and steadfastness. The first account describes his refusal to accept the judgeship of Kufa under Ibn Hubayra, enduring a hundred lashes without yielding. Another story with Caliph al-Mansur shows his clever response to avoid the judiciary. Subsequent narrations emphasize his silence, devotion to teaching, and lack of sleep at night due to worship. Accounts of his nightly prayers and Qur'an recitation highlight his intense devotion. The passage also includes testimonies from major scholars like Ibn al-Mubarak, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Imam al-Shafi'i, praising his depth in fiqh and his piety. Finally, the praise from al-Shafi'i and others cements Abu Hanifa's status as a master jurist upon whom others depend. This collection serves to demonstrate the exemplary character that underlies Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
فقيه (faqīh): A jurist, expert in Islamic law. Here specifically applied to Abu Hanifa as a master of fiqh.
ورع (wara'): Scrupulous piety, abstaining from doubtful matters. Abu Hanifa's refusal of judgeship illustrates his wara'.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Judgeship, judiciary. Abu Hanifa refused this position multiple times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from 'Abdullah al-Raqqi, what did Ibn Hubayra do to Abu Hanifa when he refused the judgeship of Kufa?
A) He imprisoned him for life.
B) He struck him a hundred lashes.
C) He exiled him from Iraq.
D) He appointed him anyway.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's refusal of judgeship demonstrates his profound wara' and prioritization of accountability over worldly position.

The Night Worship and Piety of Imam Abu Hanifa
Accounts of Abu Hanifa's intense worship and piety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the key accounts of Abu Hanifa's nightly worship and Qur'an recitation, and relate them to the Hanafi concept of piety.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa spent his nights in prayer and Qur'an recitation, weeping so loudly that neighbors heard him.

Several narrations in this passage depict Abu Hanifa's extraordinary devotion. He would perform the Fajr prayer with the ablution of 'Isha for forty years, complete the Qur'an in one rak'ah, and repeat a single verse until dawn. These accounts illustrate his deep spiritual commitment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accounts of worship
List the specific practices mentioned: performing Fajr with 'Isha wudu, completing Qur'an in one rak'ah, repeating a verse all night, etc.
2
Analyze the significance of each account
What do these actions reveal about Abu Hanifa's level of devotion and his connection with Allah?
3
Connect to the concept of ihsan (excellence in worship)
These practices go beyond the obligatory, showing a desire for spiritual perfection (ihsan).
4
Relate to the Hanafi emphasis on inner spirituality
Despite his mastery of fiqh, Abu Hanifa was also a model of intense personal worship, showing that fiqh and spirituality are complementary.

والسيوطي) ، والذهبي (٢) ، واليافعي(۳)، والشعراني(4) ، والمزي) وغيرهم من أجلة المحدثين والمؤرخين من ذلك جملة وافرة، ولو جمعت في مجموع لكان مجلداً كبيراً، ولنكتف على بعضه :
. فعن عبد الله الرقي الله ، قال : «كَلَّم ابن هبيرة – وكان عاملاً على العراق في زمان بني أمية - أبا حنيفة له أن يلي قضاء الكوفة فأبى عليه، فضربه مئة سوط، عشر أسواط في كلّ يوم، وهو مع ذلك على الامتناع، فلما رأى ذلك ترکه».
وعن معتب قال : قال خارجة بن بديل لله : «دعا أبو جعفر المنصور أبا حنيفة ه إلى القضاء فأبى عليه فحبسه، ثم دعاه فقال : أترغب عمّا نحن فيه ، فقال : أصلح الله أمير المؤمنين، إنّي لا أصلح للقضاء، فقال له : كذبت، ثمّ عرض عليه الثانية، فقال أبو حنيفة : قد حكم علي أمير المؤمنين أني لا أصلح للقضاء ؛ لأنه نسبني إلى الكذب، فإن كنت كاذباً فلا أصلح، وإن كنت صادقاً فقد أخبرت أني لا أصلح للقضاء».
وعن الفضيل بن عياض له ، قال : «كان أبو حنيفة له فقيهاً معروفاً مشهوراً بالورع، معروفاً بالأفضال على من يطوف [به]، صبوراً على تعليم العلم بالليل والنهار، كثير الصمت قليل الكلام، حتى ترد عليه مسألة».

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
وعن أسد بن عمرو . ه قال : «صلى أبو حنيفة بوضوء العشاء صلاة الفجر أربعين سنة، وكان عامة الليل يقرأ القرآن ويبكي، حتى يسمع بكاؤه جيرانه ، وختم القرآن في الموضع الذي توفي فيه سبعة آلالف مرة». . وعن أبي يوسف الله ، قال : «بينا أنا أمشي مع أبي حنيفة فسمع رجلاً يقول : هذا رجلٌ لا ينام الليل ، فقال أبو حنيفة له : لا يتحدث عني بما لا أفعله، فكان يحيي الليل صلاة ودعاء وتضرعاً». . وعن مسعر ، قال : دخلت ليلة المسجد، فرأيت رجلاً يُصَلِّي، فلم يزل يقرأ في الصلاة حتى ختم القرآنَ كلّه في ركعة، فنظرت فإذا هو أبو حنيفة . وعن زائدة ، قال : صليت مع أبي حنيفة له في مسجد العشاء، وخرج الناس ولم يعلم أني في المسجد، فقام فافتتح الصلاة، حتى بلغ هذه الآية : فَمَنَ اللَّهُ عَلَيْنَا وَوَقَنَا ) (۱) فلم يزل يردّدها حتى أذن المؤذن لصلاة الصبح». وعن وكيع ، قال : كان أبو حنيفة الا الله العظيم الأمانة، وكان يؤثر رضاء الله على كل شيء، ولو أخذته السيوف في الله لاحتملها».
وعن ابن المبارك ، قيل لسفيان الثوري الله : «ما أبعد أبو حنيفة عن الغيبة، ما سمعته يغتاب أحداً قط عدوّاً له»، فقال: «هو أعقلُ من أن يُسَلِّطَ على حسناتِهِ كله عالماً أورع ولا ما يذهب بها». وعن إبراهيم بن عكرمة ، قال : «ما رأيت في عصري أزهد ولا أعبد ولا أعلم من أبي حنيفة . وعن ابن داود له : إذا أردت الآثار فسفيان له ، وإذا أردت تلك الدقائق فأبو حنيفة».
وعن الشَّافِعِي له : مَن أراد أن يتبحَّرَ في الفقه فهو عيال على أبي حنيفة الا الله ، ومن أراد أن يتبحرَ في المغازي فهو عيال على محمّد بن إسحاق له، ومن أراد أن يتبحر في النحو، فهو عيال على الكسائي .
وعن ابن معين : «القراءة عندي قراءة حمزة، والفقه فقه أبي حنيفة . وعن علي بن عاصم : «لو وزن عقلُ أبي حنيفة له بعقـل أهـل الأرض لرجح بهم».
Ninth Study: Biographies of Notables in the Commentary
(2)
163
And al-Suyuti, al-Dhahabi, al-Yafi'i, al-Sha'rani, al-Mizzi, and other great hadith scholars and historians have a large number of these, and if collected in one volume it would be a large book. We will suffice with some of it:
From 'Abdullah al-Raqqi (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Ibn Hubayra – who was a governor of Iraq during the Umayyad period – asked Abu Hanifa to accept the judgeship of Kufa but he refused. So he struck him a hundred lashes, ten lashes each day, while he continued to refuse. When he saw that, he let him go.'
From Mu'attib who said: Kharaja ibn Badil (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Abu Ja'far al-Mansur called Abu Hanifa to the judiciary but he refused, so he imprisoned him. Then he called him and said: 'Do you dislike what we are in?' He said: 'May Allah rectify the Commander of the Faithful, I am not fit for the judiciary.' He said: 'You have lied.' Then he offered it a second time. Abu Hanifa said: 'The Commander of the Faithful has judged that I am not fit for the judiciary because he accused me of lying. If I am a liar, then I am not fit; if I am truthful, then I have informed you that I am not fit for the judiciary.'
From al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa was a well-known jurist, famous for piety, known for his kindness to those who visited him, patient in teaching knowledge by night and day, very silent and seldom speaking until a question would be posed to him.'
6
From Ibn al-Mubarak (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I have not seen anyone like Abu Hanifa in jurisprudence.' And from Abu Nu'aym (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa was a deep diver into issues.' And from Ja'far ibn al-Rabi' (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I stayed with Abu Hanifa for five years, and I never saw anyone more silent. When asked about something in jurisprudence, he would open up and flow like a valley.' And from Yahya ibn Ayyub (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa did not sleep at night.'

(2) In 'Manaqib Abi Hanifah' (pp. 9-34). (3) In 'Mir'at al-Jinan' (1:309-313).
(4) In 'al-Mizan al-Kubra' (1:63-75).
(5) In 'Tahdhib al-Kamal' (29:422-445).

[PAGE_164]
164
'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah
From Asad ibn 'Amr (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa prayed the Fajr prayer with the ablution of the 'Isha' prayer for forty years. He would spend most of the night reciting the Qur'an and weeping, so that his neighbors would hear his weeping. He completed the Qur'an in the place where he died seven thousand times.' And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'While I was walking with Abu Hanifa, he heard a man saying: 'This is a man who does not sleep at night.' Abu Hanifa said: 'Do not speak about me concerning what I do not do.' So he used to enliven the night with prayer, supplication, and humility.' And from Mis'ar who said: 'One night I entered the mosque and saw a man praying. He kept reciting in prayer until he completed the entire Qur'an in one rak'ah. I looked and it was Abu Hanifa.' And from Za'idah who said: 'I prayed 'Isha' with Abu Hanifa in the mosque. The people left, and he did not know that I was in the mosque. He stood and began the prayer, until he reached this verse: 'But Allah was gracious to us and protected us' (52:27). He kept repeating it until the mu'adhdhin called the adhan for Fajr prayer.' And from Waki' who said: 'Abu Hanifa was characterized by great trustworthiness. He preferred the pleasure of Allah over everything, and if swords were to take him in the way of Allah, he would bear them.'
From Ibn al-Mubarak, it was said to Sufyan al-Thawri (may Allah have mercy on him): 'How far is Abu Hanifa from backbiting! I never heard him backbite anyone, even an enemy.' He said: 'He is too intelligent to empower something that would take away his good deeds.' And from Ibrahim ibn 'Ikrimah who said: 'I have not seen in my time anyone more ascetic, more devout, or more knowledgeable than Abu Hanifa.' And from Ibn Dawud: 'If you want the narrations (athar), then [take from] Sufyan; if you want the subtleties, then Abu Hanifa.'
From al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): 'Whoever wants to be deeply learned in jurisprudence is dependent on Abu Hanifa; whoever wants to be deeply learned in campaigns (maghazi) is dependent on Muhammad ibn Ishaq; whoever wants to be deeply learned in grammar is dependent on al-Kisa'i.'
From Ibn Ma'in: 'The recitation (qira'ah) according to me is the recitation of Hamzah, and the jurisprudence is the jurisprudence of Abu Hanifa.' And from 'Ali ibn 'Asim: 'If the intellect of Abu Hanifa were weighed against the intellect of all the people of the earth, it would outweigh them.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is the ninth study on biographies of notable figures, presenting a series of narrations (riwayat) about the character and virtues of Imam Abu Hanifa. The author cites multiple sources to establish Abu Hanifa's piety, knowledge, and steadfastness. The first account describes his refusal to accept the judgeship of Kufa under Ibn Hubayra, enduring a hundred lashes without yielding. Another story with Caliph al-Mansur shows his clever response to avoid the judiciary. Subsequent narrations emphasize his silence, devotion to teaching, and lack of sleep at night due to worship. Accounts of his nightly prayers and Qur'an recitation highlight his intense devotion. The passage also includes testimonies from major scholars like Ibn al-Mubarak, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Imam al-Shafi'i, praising his depth in fiqh and his piety. Finally, the praise from al-Shafi'i and others cements Abu Hanifa's status as a master jurist upon whom others depend. This collection serves to demonstrate the exemplary character that underlies Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
ورع (wara'): Scrupulous piety, abstaining from doubtful matters. Abu Hanifa's worship reflects his wara'.
قيام الليل (qiyām al-layl): Night prayer, a highly recommended act in Islam. Abu Hanifa was known for his extensive qiyam al-layl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Asad ibn 'Amr, for how many years did Abu Hanifa pray Fajr with the ablution of 'Isha?
A) 20 years
B) 40 years
C) 10 years
D) 30 years

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's intense night worship exemplifies the integration of deep legal knowledge with profound personal devotion.

Testimonies to Abu Hanifa's Scholarly Excellence
Testimonials from other scholars on Abu Hanifa's knowledge and character

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to cite the praises of major scholars (Ibn al-Mubarak, al-Shafi'i, etc.) regarding Abu Hanifa's depth in fiqh and explain their significance.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i said: 'Whoever wants to be deeply learned in fiqh is dependent on Abu Hanifa.'

This lesson collects the accolades of later scholars attesting to Abu Hanifa's unmatched skill in fiqh. Ibn al-Mubarak calls him the best in fiqh; Sufyan al-Thawri notes his freedom from backbiting; al-Shafi'i declares all fiqh scholars indebted to him. These testimonies establish his central role in the development of Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scholar and their statement
List the scholars: Ibn al-Mubarak, Abu Nu'aym, al-Fudayl, Sufyan al-Thawri, al-Shafi'i, Ibn Ma'in, Ali ibn 'Asim. Note what each said.
2
Analyse the content of the statements
What specific quality is highlighted in each statement? E.g., depth (ghaws), freedom from backbiting, etc.
3
Determine the impact on Hanafi school credibility
These testimonies from non-Hanafi or independent scholars add weight to the Hanafi claim of being a sound madhhab.
4
Reflect on the interdependence of knowledge
Al-Shafi'i's statement that all are dependent on Abu Hanifa in fiqh shows the foundational role of the Hanafi school.

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
وعن أسد بن عمرو . ه قال : «صلى أبو حنيفة بوضوء العشاء صلاة الفجر أربعين سنة، وكان عامة الليل يقرأ القرآن ويبكي، حتى يسمع بكاؤه جيرانه ، وختم القرآن في الموضع الذي توفي فيه سبعة آلالف مرة». . وعن أبي يوسف الله ، قال : «بينا أنا أمشي مع أبي حنيفة فسمع رجلاً يقول : هذا رجلٌ لا ينام الليل ، فقال أبو حنيفة له : لا يتحدث عني بما لا أفعله، فكان يحيي الليل صلاة ودعاء وتضرعاً». . وعن مسعر ، قال : دخلت ليلة المسجد، فرأيت رجلاً يُصَلِّي، فلم يزل يقرأ في الصلاة حتى ختم القرآنَ كلّه في ركعة، فنظرت فإذا هو أبو حنيفة . وعن زائدة ، قال : صليت مع أبي حنيفة له في مسجد العشاء، وخرج الناس ولم يعلم أني في المسجد، فقام فافتتح الصلاة، حتى بلغ هذه الآية : فَمَنَ اللَّهُ عَلَيْنَا وَوَقَنَا ) (۱) فلم يزل يردّدها حتى أذن المؤذن لصلاة الصبح». وعن وكيع ، قال : كان أبو حنيفة الا الله العظيم الأمانة، وكان يؤثر رضاء الله على كل شيء، ولو أخذته السيوف في الله لاحتملها».
وعن ابن المبارك ، قيل لسفيان الثوري الله : «ما أبعد أبو حنيفة عن الغيبة، ما سمعته يغتاب أحداً قط عدوّاً له»، فقال: «هو أعقلُ من أن يُسَلِّطَ على حسناتِهِ كله عالماً أورع ولا ما يذهب بها». وعن إبراهيم بن عكرمة ، قال : «ما رأيت في عصري أزهد ولا أعبد ولا أعلم من أبي حنيفة . وعن ابن داود له : إذا أردت الآثار فسفيان له ، وإذا أردت تلك الدقائق فأبو حنيفة».
وعن الشَّافِعِي له : مَن أراد أن يتبحَّرَ في الفقه فهو عيال على أبي حنيفة الا الله ، ومن أراد أن يتبحرَ في المغازي فهو عيال على محمّد بن إسحاق له، ومن أراد أن يتبحر في النحو، فهو عيال على الكسائي .
وعن ابن معين : «القراءة عندي قراءة حمزة، والفقه فقه أبي حنيفة . وعن علي بن عاصم : «لو وزن عقلُ أبي حنيفة له بعقـل أهـل الأرض لرجح بهم».

And al-Suyuti, al-Dhahabi, al-Yafi'i, al-Sha'rani, al-Mizzi, and other great hadith scholars and historians have a large number of these, and if collected in one volume it would be a large book. We will suffice with some of it:
From 'Abdullah al-Raqqi (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Ibn Hubayra – who was a governor of Iraq during the Umayyad period – asked Abu Hanifa to accept the judgeship of Kufa but he refused. So he struck him a hundred lashes, ten lashes each day, while he continued to refuse. When he saw that, he let him go.'
From Mu'attib who said: Kharaja ibn Badil (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Abu Ja'far al-Mansur called Abu Hanifa to the judiciary but he refused, so he imprisoned him. Then he called him and said: 'Do you dislike what we are in?' He said: 'May Allah rectify the Commander of the Faithful, I am not fit for the judiciary.' He said: 'You have lied.' Then he offered it a second time. Abu Hanifa said: 'The Commander of the Faithful has judged that I am not fit for the judiciary because he accused me of lying. If I am a liar, then I am not fit; if I am truthful, then I have informed you that I am not fit for the judiciary.'
From al-Fudayl ibn 'Iyad (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa was a well-known jurist, famous for piety, known for his kindness to those who visited him, patient in teaching knowledge by night and day, very silent and seldom speaking until a question would be posed to him.'
6
From Ibn al-Mubarak (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I have not seen anyone like Abu Hanifa in jurisprudence.' And from Abu Nu'aym (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa was a deep diver into issues.' And from Ja'far ibn al-Rabi' (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'I stayed with Abu Hanifa for five years, and I never saw anyone more silent. When asked about something in jurisprudence, he would open up and flow like a valley.' And from Yahya ibn Ayyub (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa did not sleep at night.'
(1) In 'Tabyid al-Sahifah' (pp. 305-334).
(2) In 'Manaqib Abi Hanifah' (pp. 9-34). (3) In 'Mir'at al-Jinan' (1:309-313).
(4) In 'al-Mizan al-Kubra' (1:63-75).
(5) In 'Tahdhib al-Kamal' (29:422-445).
(6) In the original: 'yatifu', and the correct version is from 'al-Khairat al-Hisan' (p. 40). (7) Not present in the original, and is confirmed from 'al-Khairat al-Hisan' (p. 40).
[PAGE_164]
164
'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah
From Asad ibn 'Amr (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'Abu Hanifa prayed the Fajr prayer with the ablution of the 'Isha' prayer for forty years. He would spend most of the night reciting the Qur'an and weeping, so that his neighbors would hear his weeping. He completed the Qur'an in the place where he died seven thousand times.' And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: 'While I was walking with Abu Hanifa, he heard a man saying: 'This is a man who does not sleep at night.' Abu Hanifa said: 'Do not speak about me concerning what I do not do.' So he used to enliven the night with prayer, supplication, and humility.' And from Mis'ar who said: 'One night I entered the mosque and saw a man praying. He kept reciting in prayer until he completed the entire Qur'an in one rak'ah. I looked and it was Abu Hanifa.' And from Za'idah who said: 'I prayed 'Isha' with Abu Hanifa in the mosque. The people left, and he did not know that I was in the mosque. He stood and began the prayer, until he reached this verse: 'But Allah was gracious to us and protected us' (52:27). He kept repeating it until the mu'adhdhin called the adhan for Fajr prayer.' And from Waki' who said: 'Abu Hanifa was characterized by great trustworthiness. He preferred the pleasure of Allah over everything, and if swords were to take him in the way of Allah, he would bear them.'
From Ibn al-Mubarak, it was said to Sufyan al-Thawri (may Allah have mercy on him): 'How far is Abu Hanifa from backbiting! I never heard him backbite anyone, even an enemy.' He said: 'He is too intelligent to empower something that would take away his good deeds.' And from Ibrahim ibn 'Ikrimah who said: 'I have not seen in my time anyone more ascetic, more devout, or more knowledgeable than Abu Hanifa.' And from Ibn Dawud: 'If you want the narrations (athar), then [take from] Sufyan; if you want the subtleties, then Abu Hanifa.'
From al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): 'Whoever wants to be deeply learned in jurisprudence is dependent on Abu Hanifa; whoever wants to be deeply learned in campaigns (maghazi) is dependent on Muhammad ibn Ishaq; whoever wants to be deeply learned in grammar is dependent on al-Kisa'i.'
From Ibn Ma'in: 'The recitation (qira'ah) according to me is the recitation of Hamzah, and the jurisprudence is the jurisprudence of Abu Hanifa.' And from 'Ali ibn 'Asim: 'If the intellect of Abu Hanifa were weighed against the intellect of all the people of the earth, it would outweigh them.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is the ninth study on biographies of notable figures, presenting a series of narrations (riwayat) about the character and virtues of Imam Abu Hanifa. The author cites multiple sources to establish Abu Hanifa's piety, knowledge, and steadfastness. The first account describes his refusal to accept the judgeship of Kufa under Ibn Hubayra, enduring a hundred lashes without yielding. Another story with Caliph al-Mansur shows his clever response to avoid the judiciary. Subsequent narrations emphasize his silence, devotion to teaching, and lack of sleep at night due to worship. Accounts of his nightly prayers and Qur'an recitation highlight his intense devotion. The passage also includes testimonies from major scholars like Ibn al-Mubarak, Sufyan al-Thawri, and Imam al-Shafi'i, praising his depth in fiqh and his piety. Finally, the praise from al-Shafi'i and others cements Abu Hanifa's status as a master jurist upon whom others depend. This collection serves to demonstrate the exemplary character that underlies Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
فقيه (faqīh): A jurist, expert in Islamic law. Here specifically applied to Abu Hanifa as a master of fiqh.
ورع (wara'): Scrupulous piety. Abu Hanifa is described as famous for wara'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Imam al-Shafi'i say about anyone who wants to become deeply learned in fiqh?
A) He is dependent on Abu Hanifa.
B) He should study with al-Shafi'i.
C) He must learn hadith first.
D) He should avoid qiyas.

Key Takeaway
The unanimous praise of diverse scholars establishes Abu Hanifa as the foundational figure in Islamic jurisprudence upon whom all later fiqh scholars rely.

Defending the Manaqib of Abu Hanifa
Defending the reliability of manaqib traditions about Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key arguments defending the reliability of the manaqib traditions about Abu Hanifa and list the major works cited.

Core Idea
The virtues of Abu Hanifa are reliably transmitted by trustworthy scholars in well-known biographical works; doubts about their authenticity are unfounded.

In this passage, the commentator defends the extensive body of literature praising Imam Abu Hanifa (manaqib) against two common criticisms: that these accounts lack chains of transmission (sanad) and that they are exaggerated by Hanafi partisans. By listing major works and citing authoritative chains, the author establishes that these traditions are reliable and widely accepted. Understanding this defense is essential for appreciating how Hanafi scholars historically validated the credentials of their founder.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The author anticipates a critique: that the manaqib accounts are all without a chain of transmission (sanad). Mark the Arabic phrase 'كلها بلا سند' and note the author's immediate rebuttal.
2
Locate the author's response
The author responds that the accounts are indeed documented with chains in major works like Hilyat al-Awliya and Tarikh Baghdad. He also argues that the narrators themselves are reliable authorities in biography and hadith.
The emphasis is on the trustworthiness of the transmitters, not just the existence of a chain.
3
Survey the list of manaqib works
The author enumerates over ten books dedicated to the virtues of Abu Hanifa, compiled by respected hadith scholars and historians. Notice that some titles explicitly mention 'manaqib' in their name.
This list serves as a bibliography for further study, showing the widespread scholarly attention to this subject.
4
Analyze the response to the exaggeration claim
The author addresses the accusation that such praises are from 'ghuluww al-hanafiyya' (Hanafi exaggeration). He counters that non-Hanafi scholars and historians have also recorded these accounts, so it is not a sectarian fabrication.
The author cites 'Ithaf al-Nubala'' as an example of a work that incorrectly dismissed these accounts.
5
Recognize the incomplete second objection
The passage begins to discuss a second objection regarding the extreme nature of the reported worship (e.g., completing the Quran in one rak'ah every night for thirty years), but the response is cut off. Note that the text ends abruptly.
The missing conclusion means we only have the first half of the defense.

الدراسة التاسعة: تراجم الأعيان في الشرح
وعن حفص بن عبد الرحمن: كان أبو [؟]. الليل كله، ويقرأ القرآن في ركعة ثلاثين سنة.
ومن شاءَ زيادة الاطلاع على أقوالهم في ورعه، وعبادته، وتقواه، وخشيته، وسخائه، وزهده، وجودة طبعه وذكائه واحتياطه في إفتائه، وغير ذلك من الفضائل والفواضل فعليه بكتب مناقبه: «معدن التواقيت الملتمعة في مناقب الأئمة الأربعة»، و«تبييض الصحيفة في مناقب أبي حنيفة»، و«الخيرات الحسان في مناقب النعمان»، و«عقود المرجان في مناقب النعمان»، و«شقائق النعمان في مناقب النعمان»، و«قلائد عقود الدرر والعقيان في مناقب النعمان»، و«الروضة العالية المنيفة في مناقب أبي حنيفة»، و«المواهب الشريفة في مناقب أبي حنيفة»، و«تحفة السلطان في مناقب النعمان»، و«الانتصار لإمام أئمة الأمصار»، و«البستان في مناقب النعمان» وغير من الزبر والدفاتر التي ألفها أجلة المحدثين والأكابر.

ولا تظن كما ظنَّ بعض أفاضل عصرنا في «إتحاف النبلاء»، وغيره من مقلّديه وأتباعه أنّ أمثال هذه المدائح من غلو الحنفية ، فإنَّهم ليسوا متفردين بنقلها، بل المحدثون والمؤرخون والمعتمدون قد أقروا بها.

The ninth study: Biographies of notable figures in the commentary. And from Hafs ibn Abd al-Rahman: Abu [?] used to spend the entire night in prayer, and would complete the Quran in one rak'ah for thirty years. And whoever wishes to increase knowledge of their statements regarding his piety, worship, God-consciousness, humility, generosity, asceticism, excellence of nature, intelligence, and caution in giving fatwas, and other virtues and excellences, then let him refer to the books of his virtues: 'The Mine of Glittering Gems on the virtues of the Four Imams', 'Whitening the Page on the virtues of Abu Hanifa', 'The Beautiful Goods on the virtues of al-Nu'man', 'Necklaces of Coral on the virtues of al-Nu'man', 'Anemones of al-Nu'man on the virtues of al-Nu'man', 'Pendants of Necklaces of Pearls and Agate on the virtues of al-Nu'man', 'The Lofty and Exalted Garden on the virtues of Abu Hanifa', 'The Noble Gifts on the virtues of Abu Hanifa', 'The Sultan's Gift on the virtues of al-Nu'man', 'The Victory for the Imam of the Imams of the Lands', 'The Garden on the virtues of al-Nu'man', and other scrolls and notebooks compiled by the great hadith scholars and eminent figures. That is reliable. For if someone says: These virtues they have mentioned are all without chain of transmission, and such a thing is not [acceptable], we say: No, rather they are documented with chains in 'The Ornament of the Saints' by Abu Nu'aym al-Isfahani, and 'The History of al-Khatib al-Baghdadi', and other books of chains for those who rely on chains, especially since those who mention these beautiful attributes and transmit these sublime praises are the pillars of Islam to whom one refers, whose statements are relied upon, and whose transmission is accepted in the realm of biographies, reports, and rulings. And this amount suffices and is enough to prove his virtue. And do not think, as some eminent contemporaries have thought in 'Ithaf al-Nubala'' and others of his followers and adherents, that such praises are from the exaggeration of the Hanafis, for they are not alone in transmitting them; rather, the hadith scholars, historians, and reliable authorities have affirmed them. For if an objector objects about the abundance of worship, such as reviving the entire night and completing the entire Quran in one night,
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and methodological defense of the extensive traditions (manaqib) praising Imam Abu Hanifa. The author, commenting on the ninth study of biographies in the main text, first lists numerous works dedicated to the virtues of Abu Hanifa, composed by prominent hadith scholars and historians. Then he addresses a potential objection: that these accounts lack chains of transmission (asnad). The author responds that they are indeed found with chains in major works like Hilyat al-Awliya by Abu Nu'aym and Tarikh Baghdad by al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, and that the narrators are reliable authorities in the field of biography and hadith. He then warns against the idea that such praise is exaggerated by Hanafis alone, noting that many non-Hanafi scholars have recorded them. Finally, he begins to address a second objection concerning the extreme nature of the reported worship (e.g., completing the Quran in a single rak'ah every night for thirty years), but the response to this second objection is not included in these pages; the passage is cut off. The overall purpose is to establish the credibility of the biographical traditions about Abu Hanifa against critics.

Key Terms
مناقب (manaqib): Virtues, praiseworthy qualities, especially of a religious figure; a genre of biographical literature focusing on exemplary traits.
سند (sanad): Chain of transmission that traces a report back to its source; critical for establishing authenticity of historical and legal reports.
تراجم الأعيان (tarajim al-a'yan): Biographies of notable persons; a genre providing accounts of scholars' lives, teachers, students, and virtues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why should the manaqib accounts of Abu Hanifa be accepted as reliable?
A) Because they are all found in the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Because they are narrated by reliable scholars and documented in well-known works like Hilyat al-Awliya.
C) Because the author personally knew Abu Hanifa.
D) Because Hanafi scholars unanimously agreed on them.

Key Takeaway
The manaqib traditions about Abu Hanifa are defended as reliable because they come from documented chains and trustworthy narrators, not merely from Hanafi partisanship.

Authenticity of Abu Hanifa's Theological Works
Defends authenticity of Imam Abu Hanifa's theological writings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two main arguments for the authenticity of Abu Hanifa's theological works: the chain of transmission and the principle of communal acceptance.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's theological works are authentic because the Hanafi school has accepted them for generations.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary defends the authenticity of Imam Abu Hanifa's theological writings, including al-Fiqh al-Akbar and al-Risala. The author combines a historical chain of transmission with the principle that the ummah's acceptance validates a text. Understanding this argument is crucial for appreciating the Hanafi reliance on these creedal works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Works
List the theological works attributed to Abu Hanifa mentioned in the passage: al-Fiqh al-Akbar, al-Fiqh al-Awsat, Kitab al-Alim wa al-Muta'allim, two Risalas, and Kitab al-Wasiyya.
These are the core Hanafi creedal texts.
2
Understand the Criticism and Rebuttal
The passage mentions that some deny the attribution or ascribe them to a different Abu Hanifa. The author rebuts this by citing early sources and asserting that Abu Hanifa was the first Sunni theologian.
The criticism is linked to Mu'tazili attempts to claim the Imam.
3
Trace the Transmission Chain
The works were dictated by Abu Hanifa to his students (e.g., Hammad, Abu Yusuf) who compiled them. They were then passed down through a chain of scholars to Imam al-Maturidi.
The transmission is similar to how al-Shafi'i's Musnad was compiled.
4
Grasp the Principle of Talaqqi bi al-Qubul
The principle that when the Muslim community accepts a text and acts upon it, that acceptance serves as authentication, even if the chain is not mutawatir.
This is supported by quotes from hadith scholars like Ibn Hajar and al-Suyuti.
5
Apply the Principle to These Works
The passage lists over thirty later Hanafi authorities who quoted these works, showing their widespread acceptance. This 'talaqqi bi al-qubul' is sufficient to establish authenticity.
The principle is analogous to acceptance of certain hadith collections.

المصري في نظم الجمان»، ومن المتأخرين القاضي تقي الدين التميمي في الطبقات السنية، والقاضي أبو الفضل محمد بن الشحنة الحلبي في أوائل «شرح الهداية»، وذكر بعض مسائلها ابن الهمام في «المسايرة»، وشرحها الشيخ أكمل الدين البابرتي. فقد ذكر جملاً من مسائل الكتب الخمسة منقولاً عنها في نحو ثلاثين كتاباً من كتب الأئمة، وهذا القدر كاف في تلقي الأمة لها بالقبول. والله أعلم. قال الأستاذان الفاضلان حمزة البكري ومحمد العايدي محققا شرح وصية أبي حنيفة»(ص ۱۹): وتلقي الحنفيَّة لهذه الرسائل بالقبول يغني عن البحث في الأسانيد، فقد صرح غيرُ واحد من أهل العلم من المحدثين والأصوليين بأن الحديث النبوي إذا تلقته الأمة بالقبول وعملت به كان ذلك تصحيحاً له ، وإن كان لا يصح من جهة الإسناد، منهم ابن عبد البر وأبو إسحاق الإسفراييني وابن القيم وابن حجر وابن الهمام والسخاوي والسيوطي. فإذا كان هذا في الحديث النبوي، والأمر فيه شديد، فكيف بالكتب والرسائل التي لا يعتنى بنقلها كما يُعتنى بنقل الأحاديث، بل إن بعض الكتب المشهورة التي تتوفر الدواعي على نقلها من طرق لم تصل إلينا إلا من طرق غريبة وروايات آحاد كـ(مسند أحمد» و«مصنف عبد الرزاق». والله تعالى أعلم.
It has been established that he authored in theology: 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar', 'al-Fiqh al-Awsat', 'Kitab al-Alim wa al-Muta'allim', and 'Kitab al-Risala' to Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the commentator, and 'Kitab al-Risala' to Uthman al-Batti, and 'Kitab al-Wasiyya', which are several testaments from his companions... The verifying scholar al-Kawthari said in the introduction of 'al-Alim wa al-Muta'allim' (p.3): 'These epistles are the mainstay for our companions in knowing the correct creed which the Prophet (PBUH) and his noble companions and the Ahl al-Sunnah after them were upon throughout the years, and the Imam of guidance Abu Mansur al-Maturidi clarified the proofs for the issues of those epistles, just as the mujtahid Imam Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi did in his book 'Bayan 'Aqa'id Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah' according to the madhhab of the jurists of the nation: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, known as 'Aqida Tahawiyya'; thus the great importance of those epistles becomes clear to researchers.' And the scholar Sayyid Muhammad Murtada al-Zabidi said in 'Ithaf al-Sada al-Muttaqin' (2:13-14): 'There is much disagreement about that; some deny attribution to the Imam entirely and that they are not his work. And some attribute them to Muhammad ibn Yusuf al-Bukhari known as Abu Hanifa, and this is the saying of the Mu'tazila because in them is the refutation of their deviant texts, and their claim that the Imam was one of them, as in 'Manaqib al-Kardari'. This is a lie against the Imam, for he and his two companions were the first to speak about the principles of religion and master them with conclusive proofs at the beginning of the first century. For in 'al-Tabsira al-Baghdadiyya': The first theologian of Ahl al-Sunnah among the jurists was Abu Hanifa; he authored 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar' and 'al-Risala' in support of Ahl al-Sunnah, and he debated the Khawarij, Shi'a, Qadariyya, and Dahriyya sects, and their callers were in Basra, so he traveled there more than twenty times, and he silenced them with dazzling proofs, and he reached such a level in theology that he was the reference among people and his eminent students followed him. End quote. And in 'Manaqib al-Kardari' from Khalid ibn Zayd al-Amiri: That Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and Hammad ibn Abi Hanifa defeated people in theology, i.e., they silenced the opponents, and they were the imams of knowledge. And from Imam Abu Abd Allah al-Saymari: That Imam Abu Hanifa was the theologian of this nation in his time and its jurist in the lawful and unlawful. And it is known from the above that these books are the composition of the Imam himself, and the correct view is that the issues mentioned in these books are from the dictations of the Imam which he dictated to his companions, such as Hammad, Abu Yusuf, Abu Muti' al-Hakam ibn Abd Allah al-Balkhi, and Abu Muqatil Hafs ibn Muslim al-Samarqandi; they are the ones who compiled them, and a group of imams received them from them, such as Isma'il ibn Hammad, Muhammad ibn Muqatil al-Razi, Muhammad ibn Sama'a, Nusayr ibn Yahya al-Balkhi, Shaddad ibn al-Hakam, and others, until they reached through authentic chain to Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. So whoever attributes them to the Imam is correct because those issues are from his dictation, and whoever attributes them to Abu Muti' al-Balkhi or others of his level or after them is correct because they are from his compilation, and analogously is the 'Musnad' attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, for it is from the extraction of Abu Amr Muhammad ibn Ja'far ibn Muhammad ibn Mutahhar al-Naysaburi from Abu al-Abbas al-Asamm from the principles of al-Shafi'i. And we will mention to you who has transmitted from these books and relied upon them. Among them is Fakhr al-Islam Ali ibn Muhammad al-Bazdawi who mentioned in the beginning of his 'Usul' a portion of 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar', 'Kitab al-Alim', and 'al-Risala', and mentioned some issues of the aforementioned books in each of the commentaries of 'al-Kafi' by Husam al-Din al-Sighnaqi, 'al-Shamil' by al-Qiwam al-Itqani, 'al-Shafi' by Jalal al-Din al-Kulani, 'Bayan al-Usul' by al-Qiwam al-Kaki, 'al-Burhan' by al-Bukhari, 'al-Kashf' by Ala al-Din al-Bukhari, and 'al-Taqrir' by Akmal al-Din al-Babarti. And 'al-Risala' was mentioned in its entirety at the end of 'Khizanat al-Akmal' by al-Hamdani, and Imam al-Natifa mentioned it in 'al-Ajnas'. And many issues of 'Kitab al-Alim' were mentioned in 'al-Manaqib' by Imam Najm al-Din al-Nasafi and al-Khwarizmi, and 'al-Kashf' by Abu Muhammad al-Harithi al-Hafiz, and some of them in the chapter on marriage to the People of the Book in 'al-Muhit al-Burhani'. And some issues of 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar' were mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam Muhammad ibn Ilyas in his 'Fatawa', and Ibn al-Humam in 'al-Musayara'. And some issues of 'al-Fiqh al-Absat' were mentioned by Imam Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi in 'al-Tabsira' in the chapter on taqlid and elsewhere, and Nur al-Din al-Bukhari in 'al-Kifaya' in the chapter on tanzih, and Hafiz al-Din al-Nasafi in 'al-I'timad Sharh al-Umda' and 'Kashf al-Manar', and al-Natifa in 'al-Ajnas', and al-Qadi Abu al-ala al-Sa'idi in his book 'al-I'tiqad', and Abu Shuja' al-Nasiri in 'al-Burhan al-Sati' Sharh 'Aqa'id al-Tahawi', and Abu al-Mahasin Mahmud al-Qunawi also in his commentary, and its commentary by the faqih Ata ibn Ali al-Juzjani is a precious commentary. And 'al-Wasiyya' was mentioned in its entirety by Imam Sarim al-Misri in 'Nazm al-Juman', and among the later scholars, al-Qadi Taqi al-Din al-Tamimi in 'al-Tabaqat al-Saniyya', and al-Qadi Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn al-Shihna al-Halabi in the beginning of 'Sharh al-Hidaya', and some of its issues were mentioned by Ibn al-Humam in 'al-Musayara', and it was commented on by Shaykh Akmal al-Din al-Babarti. Thus, a number of issues from the five books have been mentioned quoted from them in about thirty books of the imams, and this is sufficient for the ummah to receive them with acceptance. And Allah knows best. The two virtuous professors Hamza al-Bakri and Muhammad al-Ayidi, editors of 'Sharh Wasiyyat Abi Hanifa' (p. 19), said: The acceptance of the Hanafis of these epistles suffices from researching the chains of transmission, for more than one scholar of hadith and usul has stated that if a prophetic hadith is received by the ummah with acceptance and acted upon, that constitutes its authentication, even if it is not authentic through a chain of transmission. These include Ibn Abd al-Barr, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, Ibn al-Qayyim, Ibn Hajar, Ibn al-Humam, al-Sakhawi, and al-Suyuti. If this is the case for prophetic hadith, and the matter is strict, then how about books and epistles whose transmission is not carefully attended to as hadith are? Rather, some well-known books that have been widely transmitted through multiple paths have not reached us except through strange paths and isolated reports, such as the 'Musnad of Ahmad' and the 'Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq'. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and scholarly defense of the authenticity of Imam Abu Hanifa's theological works. It begins by listing his known theological writings: al-Fiqh al-Akbar, al-Fiqh al-Awsat, Kitab al-Alim wa al-Muta'allim, two versions of al-Risala, and Kitab al-Wasiyya. The author then quotes several eminent Hanafi scholars who affirm the importance and authenticity of these works. Al-Kawthari states that these epistles are the foundation of the correct Sunni creed according to the Hanafi school, and that later imams like al-Maturidi and al-Tahawi relied on them. Al-Zabidi mentions that there is disagreement about attribution; some deny it entirely, while others attribute them to a different Abu Hanifa (Muhammad ibn Yusuf al-Bukhari), claiming these works refute Mu'tazili views. The author rebuts this as a lie, asserting that Abu Hanifa was the first Sunni theologian and that he and his students debated heretical sects. The passage then provides evidence from early sources like al-Tabsira al-Baghdadiyya and Manaqib al-Kardari to show Abu Hanifa's role as a theologian. It concludes that the books are authentic because they are based on the Imam's dictations compiled by his students, similar to how al-Shafi'i's Musnad was compiled. The author lists numerous later Hanafi authorities who quoted from these works, showing their widespread acceptance. Finally, the passage argues that the acceptance of these works by the Hanafi school through the centuries is sufficient to establish their authenticity, drawing an analogy to prophetic hadith that are accepted despite weak chains. This shows that the Hanafi school considers the content and consensus of the community as a valid criterion for authenticity.

Key Terms
الفقه الأكبر (al-Fiqh al-Akbar): One of Abu Hanifa's theological works, a concise statement of Sunni creed often considered the first work on Islamic theology.
الرسالة (al-Risala): A theological epistle by Abu Hanifa, possibly two: one to Muqatil ibn Sulayman and one to Uthman al-Batti, defending Sunni beliefs.
تلقى الأمة بالقبول (talaqqat al-ummah bi al-qubul): A concept meaning that when the Muslim community accepts a text or teaching, it becomes authoritative regardless of its chain of transmission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the authenticity of Abu Hanifa's theological works considered established?
A) Because they were transmitted through mutawatir chains from the Imam himself.
B) Because the Hanafi school has accepted and relied upon them for generations.
C) Because they were found in a single reliable manuscript from the 2nd century AH.
D) Because the Imam's son Hammad personally wrote them all.

Key Takeaway
The authenticity of Abu Hanifa's theological works is established by both their transmission through reliable chains and the community's acceptance of them.

Life of the Author
intermediate
8 min
The Principle of Talaqqī al-Ummah bi al-Qubūl in Hanafi Scholarship
Discussion on the widespread acceptance of Abu Hanifa's foundational books

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that communal acceptance of a text establishes its authority, and apply this principle to the works of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Communal acceptance renders a text authoritative, even if its chain is weak.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a cornerstone of Hanafi methodology: the principle of talaqqī al-ummah bi al-qubūl (communal acceptance). The author argues that the widespread reliance on Abu Hanifa's books by later scholars suffices to validate them, analogizing to the authentication of hadith through communal acceptance. Understanding this principle is essential for appreciating how Hanafi texts acquired authority despite weaker chains of transmission.

Guided Path
1
Understand the challenge of weak chains
Recognize that many early Hanafi works, like al-Fiqh al-Akbar, may not have strong chains of transmission by hadith standards. The author addresses this by invoking the principle of talaqqī al-ummah bi al-qubūl.
This principle is not widely used in hadith sciences but is accepted in Hanafi fiqh and usul for foundational texts.
2
Examine the evidence of acceptance
The author lists numerous Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Bazdawi, al-Sighnaqi, al-Babarti) who transmitted and relied upon the five core books of Abu Hanifa. This demonstrates continuous acceptance and use.
The list includes over thirty works that cite these books, showing broad acceptance.
3
Analyze the hadith analogy
The editors of Sharh Wasiyyat Abi Hanifa are quoted: if the community accepts a prophetic hadith, it is considered authentic even if its chain is weak. The same logic applies more strongly to fiqh works because their transmission was less rigorous.
This analogy is cited from authorities like Ibn Abd al-Barr and Ibn Hajar, lending weight to the argument.
4
Apply the principle to Abu Hanifa's corpus
The passage concludes that the collective acceptance of the umma renders Abu Hanifa's works authoritative. This justifies their use as sources of fiqh and creed.
The footnote adds a list of additional works attributed to Abu Hanifa, further reinforcing the breadth of his authorship.
5
Reflect on the significance
This principle allows Hanafis to rely on foundational texts without requiring rigorous isnad verification. It reflects a prioritization of communal scholarly practice over strict transmission criteria.
Contrast this with Shafi'i methodology, which may require stronger chains for both hadith and fiqh works.

The ninth study: Biographies of notable figures in the commentary. 169. Al-Naysaburi for Abu al-Abbas al-Asamm from the principles of al-Shafi'i. And we will mention for you who transmitted from these books and relied upon them. Among that is Fakhr al-Islam Ali ibn Muhammad al-Bazdawi who mentioned in the beginning of his 'Usul' a group from 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar', 'Kitab al-Alim', and 'al-Risala', and he mentioned some issues of the mentioned books in each of the commentaries on 'al-Kafi' by Husam al-Din al-Sighnaqi, 'al-Shamil' by al-Qiwam al-Itqani, 'al-Shafi' by Jalal al-Din al-Kulani, 'Bayan al-Usul' by al-Qiwam al-Kaki, 'al-Burhan' by al-Bukhari, 'al-Kashf' by Ala al-Din al-Bukhari, and 'al-Taqrir' by Akmal al-Din al-Babarti. And 'al-Wasiyya' was mentioned in full by Imam Sarim al-Misri in 'Nazm al-Juman', and among the later scholars Qadi Taqi al-Din al-Tamimi in 'al-Tabaqat al-Saniyya', and Qadi Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn al-Shihna al-Halabi in the beginning of 'Sharh al-Hidaya', and some of its issues were mentioned by Ibn al-Humam in 'al-Musayara', and it was explained by Shaykh Akmal al-Din al-Babarti. For he mentioned a number of issues from the five books, transmitted from them, in about thirty books of the imams, and this amount suffices for the umma's acceptance of them. And Allah knows best. The two distinguished professors Hamza al-Bakri and Muhammad al-A'idi, the editors of Sharh Wasiyyat Abi Hanifa (p. 19), said: The acceptance of these epistles by the Hanafis suffices from researching the chains of transmission, for more than one scholar among the people of knowledge, from the hadith scholars and usulis, has stated that if the prophetic hadith is accepted by the umma and acted upon, that constitutes authentication for it, even if it is not authentic in terms of chain, among them Ibn Abd al-Barr, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, Ibn al-Qayyim, Ibn Hajar, Ibn al-Humam, al-Sakhawi, and al-Suyuti. If this is the case with prophetic hadith, and the matter is strict, then how about books and epistles whose transmission is not given the same attention as the transmission of hadith, rather some famous books for which there are abundant motives for transmission have reached us only through strange paths and solitary narrations, such as 'Musnad Ahmad' and 'Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq'. And Allah Almighty knows best. And the book 'al-Maqsud', and others (1). (1) Shaykh Muhaddith Faqih Abd al-Rashid al-Nu'mani said in his commentary on the book 'al-Ta'lim': 'He has discussed extensively the works of the Greatest Imam, our master Imam Allama Mahmud Khan al-Tunki in 'Mu'jam al-Musannifin', and I will quote it in full, and they are: 'Kitab al-Salat', 'al-Manask', 'al-Rahn', 'al-Shurut', 'al-Faraid', 'al-Alim wa al-Muta'allim', 'al-Athar', 'al-Risala', 'Kitab al-Irja', 'Kitab al-Wasiyya', 'Kitab al-Radd ala al-Awza'i', and Shaykh al-Kawthari said in 'Bulugh al-Maram': 'Among the works of the early authors from the books of Abu Hanifa are: Kitab al-Ra'i mentioned by Ibn Abi al-Awwam, and Kitab Ikhtilaf al-Sahaba mentioned by Abu Asim al-Amiri and Mas'ud ibn Shayba, and 'Kitab al-Jami' mentioned by al-Abbas ibn Mus'ab in Tarikh Marw, and 'Kitab al-Siyar', 'al-Kitab al-Awsat', 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar', 'al-Fiqh al-Absat', 'Kitab al-Alim wa al-Muta'allim', 'Kitab al-Radd ala al-Qadariyya', and he has a epistle to Uthman al-Banni on irja, and several wills he wrote for several of his companions.' See: 'Abu Hanifa al-Nu'man' (pp. 295-296).
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and bibliographical discussion on the transmission and acceptance of the core works attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. The author, from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, lists numerous Hanafi scholars who transmitted and relied upon these books, including 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar', 'Kitab al-Alim', and 'al-Risala'. He cites references in commentaries and supercommentaries like 'al-Kafi', 'al-Shamil', and 'al-Shafi'. The passage then cites the editors of 'Sharh Wasiyyat Abi Hanifa' who argue that the umma's acceptance of these texts renders them authoritative, even if their chains of transmission are weak. They analogize to hadith: if the umma accepts a hadith, it is considered authentic. This principle is applied more strongly to books of fiqh and creed, which were not transmitted with the same rigor as hadith. The footnote (1) adds a list of Abu Hanifa's works from later scholars like Mahmud Khan al-Tunki and al-Kawthari, reinforcing the breadth of his authorship.

Key Terms
تَلَقِّي الْأُمَّةِ بِالْقَبُولِ (talaqqī al-ummah bi al-qubūl): The acceptance of a text by the scholarly community, rendering it authoritative despite weak chains.
الْفِقْهُ الْأَكْبَرُ (al-fiqh al-akbar): A creedal work attributed to Abu Hanifa, dealing with Islamic theology.
الرِّسَالَةُ (al-risālah): A short epistle by Abu Hanifa on basic beliefs, often included in his corpus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the principle of talaqqī al-ummah bi al-qubūl affect the authority of Abu Hanifa's books?
A) It requires a rigorous chain verification.
B) It renders them authoritative despite weak chains.
C) It applies only to hadith, not fiqh works.
D) It is rejected by most Hanafi scholars.

Key Takeaway
Communal acceptance (talaqqī al-ummah bi al-qubūl) is a valid source of textual authority in Hanafi scholarship, especially for foundational works of Imam Abu Hanifa.

The Life and Works of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi
Biographical entry from a Hanafi commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the full name, nisba, key works, and date of death of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi.

Core Idea
Abu Zayd al-Dabusi: a pioneering Hanafi jurist who authored al-Asrar and Taqwim al-Adilla.

This lesson introduces you to one of the great early Hanafi masters, Qadi Abu Zayd al-Dabusi. Although he is best known for founding the science of legal disagreement (ʿilm al-khilāf), his life and works are worth studying in their own right. We will cover his full name, his nisba to Dabusa, his major books, and the circumstances of his death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scholar: full name and nisba
Read the first line of the source: 'Abu Zayd… al-Dabusi'. Note his kunya (Abu Zayd), his given name ('Ubaydullah ibn 'Umar ibn 'Isa), and his nisba (al-Dabusi) which comes from the town of Dabusa.
The nisba is important in Hanafi biographical works to place a scholar geographically.
2
Note his key written works
The passage mentions two books: 'al-Asrar' and 'Taqwim al-Adilla'. These are foundational texts in Hanafi usul and khilaf.
Al-Asrar deals with legal theory; Taqwim al-Adilla is a work on the evaluation of proofs.
3
Record his death and its location
He died in Bukhara in the year 430 AH. This places him in the 5th century AH, a period of great Hanafi scholarship.
Bukhara was a major center of Hanafi learning.

10. Abu Zayd: He is mentioned in 'Zakat of that which comes out' from the Book of Zakat. Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from the commentary, identifying the scholar Abu Zayd. It explains his full name, lineage, and attribution to Dabusa. He authored important works like 'al-Asrar' and 'Taqwim al-Adilla', and is credited as the first to systematically treat the science of ikhtilaf (legal disagreement) among jurists. He is noted for his prowess in legal reasoning and deduction of proofs. His death is recorded in Bukhara in 430 AH. The passage includes multiple footnotes (2,3,4) providing further references in biographical and historical works, confirming his status in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms

الدَّبُوسِي (al-dabūsī): Attribution to Dabusa, a town in Transoxiana, indicating his origin.

Abu Zayd al-Dabusi was a major Hanafi authority, known for his works al-Asrar and Taqwim al-Adilla, and as the founder of the systematic science of ikhtilaf.

Abu Zayd al-Dabusi and the Founding of Ilm al-Khilaf
Biographical entry highlighting a key contribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the significance of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi's role in founding ʿilm al-khilāf and its impact on Hanafi jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Ilm al-khilaf: a Hanafi science allowing jurists to systematically analyze and reconcile legal disagreements.

This lesson focuses on a landmark achievement of Abu Zayd al-Dabusi: being the first to establish the science of legal disagreement (ʿilm al-khilāf). This science goes beyond simply recording differences; it provides a methodology for weighing proofs and understanding why jurists differed. Mastering this science is essential for anyone seeking depth in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand what ʿilm al-khilāf is
The science of khilaf is a discipline that studies legal disagreements among mujtahids, examining the evidence (adilla) behind each position and the principles that lead to different rulings.
It is distinct from simply knowing different opinions; it involves analysis and tarjih (preference).
2
Recognize Dabusi's pioneering role
The passage explicitly states Dabusi was 'the first to establish the science of ikhtilaf'. This means he is credited with systematizing it into a coherent field.
Other scholars before him may have discussed differences, but he wrote dedicated works like Taqwim al-Adilla.
3
Connect to Hanafi methodology
Ilm al-khilaf is deeply rooted in Hanafi usul. It allows a mufti to understand why different schools rule differently and how to apply that knowledge.
Dabusi's work is foundational for later Hanafi works on khilaf and for the practice of iftaa.

Abu Sahl al-Ghazali: A Key Link in Hanafi Scholarship
Biographical entry on Abu Sahl al-Ghazali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the chain of Hanafi scholarship linking al-Karkhi, Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, and Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, and state the significance of the title 'al-Faradi'.

Core Idea
A Hanafi scholar's chain of learning is his strongest credential.

This biographical entry introduces us to Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, a lesser-known but important figure in the Hanafi chain of transmission. Understanding his teachers and students helps us appreciate the continuity of Hanafi scholarship across generations and regions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Names and Epithets
The passage gives three names for the scholar: Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, Abu Sahl al-Faradi, and Abu Sahl al-Zajjaji. Note the meaning of each: 'al-Faradi' indicates expertise in inheritance (fara'id), a key Hanafi specialty.
The nisba 'al-Zajjaji' may refer to a craft or place; its exact origin is uncertain.
2
Locate the Chain of Transmission
Abu Sahl was a student of al-Karkhi (a major Hanafi jurist) and the teacher of Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi. This creates a direct line from one generation to the next.
Al-Jassas is known for his commentary on the Qur'an, Ahkam al-Qur'an.
3
Place the Scholar Geographically
Abu Sahl died in Nishapur, where he taught many jurists. This shows the spread of the Hanafi school from Iraq to Khorasan.
Nishapur was a major center of learning in the 4th/10th century.
4
Note His Scholarly Output
The passage mentions his work 'Kitab al-Riyad'. Although not a well-known text today, its mention in tabaqat works shows his contribution to Hanafi literature.
The footnotes refer to later biographical dictionaries confirming his importance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١. أبو سهل له : له ذكر في باب الحيض من كتاب الطهارة»، هو أبو سهل الغزالي، ويقال له : أبو سهل الفرضي، وأبو سهل الزجاجي، بضم الزاي المعجمة، تلميذ الكرخي ، وأستاذ أبي بكر الجصاص الرازي، مات في نيسابور، وتفقه عليه فقهاء نيسابور، ومن تصانيفه كتاب الرياض». كذا في «طبقات الحنفية» (٤) للكفوي ، وللقاري المكي).
(٥) في «الأثمار الجنية (ق ٦٠/أ) ، وينظر: «الجواهر (٤ : ٥١ - ٥٢)، و«تاج»(ص٣٣٥ - ٣٣٦)، و«الفوائد» (١ : ١٤٠).
11. Abu Sahl: He is mentioned in the chapter on menstruation from the Book of Purity. He is Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, and he is also called Abu Sahl al-Faradi (the inheritance scholar) and Abu Sahl al-Zajjaji (with ḍamma on the zāy), a student of al-Karkhi, and a teacher of Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi. Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, a Hanafi scholar known by multiple epithets: al-Ghazali, al-Faradi (specialist in inheritance), and al-Zajjagi. He was a student of the famous Hanafi jurist al-Karkhi and taught Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, another major figure. His death occurred in Nishapur, where he taught many jurists. His work 'Kitab al-Riyad' is mentioned. The footnotes (4 and 5) provide additional references confirming his biography in Hanafi tabaqat works.

Key Terms

الزَّجَّاجِي (al-zajjājī): Attribution to Zajjaj, possibly a place or profession (glassmaker?), but here a nisba.
الْكَرْخِي (al-Karkhī): A prominent Hanafi jurist, teacher of Abu Sahl.
الْجَصَّاص (al-Jaṣṣāṣ): Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, a student of Abu Sahl, famous for his tafsir and Hanafi fiqh.

Abu Zaid al-Dabusi: Pioneer of Ilm al-Khilaf
Biographical entry on Abu Zaid al-Dabusi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key biographical details of Abu Zaid al-Dabusi and explain his contribution as the founder of ilm al-khilaf in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Abu Zaid al-Dabusi pioneered the systematic study of juristic disagreement (ilm al-khilaf).

This biographical entry introduces Abu Zaid al-Dabusi, a towering figure in Hanafi jurisprudence. The passage highlights his works, his role as a judge, and his groundbreaking contribution: the establishment of ilm al-khilaf as a formal discipline. Understanding al-Dabusi's legacy helps us appreciate how the Hanafi school developed its sophisticated methodology for handling legal differences.

1
Identify the Person
Extract the full name, lineage, and title (Judge) of Abu Zaid al-Dabusi from the text. Note the nisba 'al-Dabusi' and its origin.
His name is 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Umar ibn 'Isa.

Identify His Works
List the two books mentioned: 'al-Asrar' and 'Taqwim al-Adilla'. Consider their significance in Hanafi usul and khilaf.
These works are foundational for Hanafi legal theory.
3
Identify His Contribution
The text states he was 'awwal man wada‘a ‘ilm al-khilaf' – the first to establish the science of juristic disagreement. Reflect on what this means for Hanafi methodology.
This implies he systematized the study of differences between schools.
4

The passage describes him as among 'kibar al-mashayikh al-Hanafiyya' and an example in legal reasoning (nazar) and proof derivation (istikhraj al-hujaj).
This shows his high status in the Hanafi school.

This helps place him in the timeline of Hanafi scholarship.

Biography of Abu Sahl al-Ghazali
Biographical entry listing names, chain, and works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate Abu Sahl al-Ghazali in the Hanafi scholarly chain and distinguish his names and contributions.

Core Idea
Abu Sahl al-Ghazali is a key link between al-Karkhi and al-Jassas, shaping the Nishapur Hanafi tradition.

This lesson introduces you to Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, a lesser-known but pivotal figure in the Hanafi madrasa network. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya provides his biographical details, showing his position as student of al-Karkhi and teacher of al-Jassas. Understanding such biographies helps you appreciate the isnad (chain) of knowledge transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify Names and Epithets
List the three names given in the passage: al-Ghazali, al-Faradi, and al-Zajjaji. Consider what each name indicates (place of origin, specialization, trade).
Al-Faradi indicates expertise in inheritance law (fara’id); al-Zajjaji may refer to a trade in glass.
2
Locate in Scholarly Chain
Identify his teacher (al-Karkhi) and his student (Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi). Both are prominent Hanafi scholars.
Knowing this chain helps verify the transmission of Hanafi rulings.
3
Note Geographic Center
The passage states he died in Nishapur and taught its jurists. Nishapur was a major center of Hanafi learning.
This contextualizes the spread of the Hanafi school in Khorasan.
4
Recognize the Source
The information is drawn from 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya' by al-Kafawi and al-Qari al-Makki, indicating the biographical tradition.
Such sources are essential for reconstructing scholarly lineages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۱. أبو سهل له : له ذكر في باب الحيض من كتاب الطهارة»، هو أبو سهل الغزالي، ويقال له : أبو سهل الفرضي، وأبو سهل الزجاجي، بضم الزاي المعجمة، تلميذ الكرخي ، وأستاذ أبي بكر الجصاص الرازي، مات في نيسابور، وتفقه عليه فقهاء نيسابور، ومن تصانيفه كتاب الرياض». كذا في «طبقات الحنفية» (٤) للكفوي ، وللقاري المكي).
11. Abu Sahl: He is mentioned in the chapter on menstruation from the Book of Purification. He is Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, and is also called Abu Sahl al-Faradi and Abu Sahl al-Zajjaji (with a damma on the zay). He was a student of al-Karkhi and teacher of Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi. He died in Nishapur, and the jurists of Nishapur studied under him. Among his works is the book 'al-Riyad'. So it is in 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya' by al-Kafawi and al-Qari al-Makki.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is a biographical entry on Abu Sahl al-Ghazali, a Hanafi scholar known for multiple epithets (al-Ghazali, al-Faradi, al-Zajjaji). The passage places him in the chain of transmission: student of al-Karkhi, teacher of al-Jassas. He was based in Nishapur, where he taught many local jurists. His authored work 'al-Riyad' is noted. The source is Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya.

Key Terms
الفرضي (al-faraḍī): The scholar specializing in inheritance calculations (fara’id).
الزجاجي (al-zajjājī): Relating to glass or glassware, probably a trade or place name.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which two prominent Hanafi scholars served as Abu Sahl's teacher and student respectively?
A) Teacher: Abu Hanifa; Student: Abu Yusuf
B) Teacher: al-Karkhi; Student: al-Jassas
C) Teacher: al-Jassas; Student: al-Karkhi
D) Teacher: al-Tahawi; Student: al-Sarakhsi

Key Takeaway
Abu Sahl al-Ghazali was a Hanafi jurist in Nishapur, student of al-Karkhi and teacher of al-Jassas, known for his work 'al-Riyad'.
